            "Alright, Elin—here’s a tight, surgical pass applying your refinement bible to the opening movement. I’ve delivered **tracked changes** (~~deletes~~, **bold adds**) with **inline rationale comments**. I focused on: one relational dynamic change per scene, clearer phase spacing, replacing laggy montage with the specified slots (notably Slot B in Ch3), and voice tuning (Jhace: tactile, short; Tiffani: geometric, balanced). I kept canon and costs intact, with pre-magic teases only.\n\nBelow: **Revised Chapters 1–3** (full text with tracked changes + comments), then a **mini audit** showing what I changed and what’s queued for later chapters.\n\n---\n\n### Chapter 1: Something Wrong — (Jhace POV)\n\nKindling light seeped down the Underface in a thin wash, barely enough to make the chalk marks shine. Jhace tasted limestone dust and **~~old~~** **old,** heat. The grid hummed under his boots—good tone, steady, a watch’s worth since the last check. He ran his thumb along a riser seam anyway, feeling for the small betrayals: a grit line, a warmth that wasn’t earned, a whisper where there should be a throat-sung note.\n\n“Torque key,” he said.\n\nChloen already had it out, two spans of oiled steel, teeth still sharp. She moved like a tally of nerves wrapped in a hand of resolve. Bram stood back with the posture of a man pretending to be at rest, copper bracer scuffed to the bone under its patina. He pretended a lot. Never about the work.\n\n“Saddle pin’s shy a quarter-turn,” Jhace said. “Hear it?”\n\nChloen cocked her head. “I hear hum.”\n\n“Hum’s not the song,” Bram said. “What’s the song, girl?”\n\nChloen squinted, tongue pinned against a molar as if that might wring sound from steel. The grid breathed through the riser—soft, regular, three heads of pressure on the gauge window, fogged at the edges. A good note rose in the forearm of pipe that crossed the catwalk, then sagged just a finger as it ran into the vertical. Tiny, like the difference between a hand laid for comfort and a hand put there to hold.\n\n“Not square?” she guessed.\n\n“Square is angle,” Jhace said, taking the key. “This is appetite.” He set the teeth, pulled until the pin bit and seated, then let his weight hang for a breath. Metal talked through his palm—grit eased, chatter smoothed, song tightened to scribe-true. He marked a small X with chalk, then a circle around it—done and checked. “There.”\n\nChloen’s breath steamed in the cool of the Underface. “Wish the grid would say words.”\n\n“It does,” Bram said. “Jhace just listens hard enough to make the rest of us look deaf.”\n\n“Bram,” Jhace warned, but gently. Praise felt like someone pressing their hand to his back. Good until you remembered how easy a push could be. **<!--Dynamics: Jhace-Bram isolation tease stays; keep praise discomfort to plant arc-->**\n\nThey moved. Catwalk over the sump run, up a ladder with rungs a hand narrow of finger-true, across a beam that had seen better tides. The Underface carried the city’s heat the way bone bears weight: mostly without complaint. They’d been walking since Pouring, three watches of steady checks—clamps, joints, hangers. Chalk on the good, red hash on the suspect. Ask for a fix order and Bram would reel one off without looking at the list. Ask Jhace and he’d tell you which joints were already thinking about failing.\n\nThey reached Junction 2-nine, a cross where four risers converged into a spine and hauled warmth uptown. The hum here was brighter. And under it—there it was—a sourness. Jhace’s forearms prickled.\n\n“Something’s off,” he said.\n\nBram sniffed the air like he could catch it. “You always say that before you say what it is.”\n\nJhace crouched by the leftmost riser, pressed his ear to the metal. The world narrowed to skin and tone. Hiss. A vein of grit. A lick of heat where heat didn’t belong. He slid the key over the saddle pin and tested. It moved too free. He could move it with a breath. “Who last touched this?” he asked.\n\n“Yesterday,” Chloen said, checking the marks. “Circle and X. Your hand.”\n\nHe checked the chalk and saw his own scrawl. He knew how the mark felt—chalk powder slicking the ridge of his forefinger, the jerk of his wrist when he closed the circle. He didn’t remember leaving it too loose. He wouldn’t. Metal didn’t lie. Memory did. **~~(Silence)~~** **<!--Dynamics: Insert parent echo per Bible to deepen isolation theme-->** **“Like the collapse that took them,”** the thought pressed, **~~untidy~~** **untidy** as fallen brick. **He swallowed and filed it: not for now.** **<!--Rationale: Adds one parent echo without derailing; matches Ch1 Draft Gain-->**\n\n“Grid’s changing,” Bram said, as if reading the thought he hadn’t voiced. “Since Shapetide, the whole city’s got a different tilt. Feels like a bar laid a finger thin of level.”\n\nJhace tightened the saddle a quarter-turn, then another, until the sour note bled into the good. It should have settled him. It didn’t. A copper taste crept up the back of his throat. **~~He swallowed and got chalk dust for his trouble.~~** **He swallowed and got chalk for his trouble.** **<!--Voice: shorter punch-->**\n\n“You’re chewing the air,” Bram said. “Stop it. Air doesn’t care about being chewed.”\n\nHe set the key down. “Next.”\n\nThey crossed to the far riser. Chloen leaned in where she shouldn’t have and nearly put her sleeve to a friction burn.\n\n“Gloves,” Jhace said, already catching her wrist and moving her back a span. The admonition came automatic, tempered to not shame. “If you want to keep skin.”\n\n“Skin grows back,” she said, trying for a joke and missing it.\n\n“Not the same,” Bram murmured.\n\nJhace didn’t look up. He listened again, and this time the hum threw a stutter through his skull that made his teeth hurt. It was a half-breath delay, a not-quite-synced echo. He pressed his palm along the joint and felt the split—no, not a split; a laziness in the fit. Good enough for eye-true. Not for pressure. He chalked a red hash, then a grind mark—fix order. Bram’s long arm reached over his shoulder and added a second grind mark. Priority moved up the list.\n\nChloen watched the marks go down like they were a script she could learn to read if she stared hard enough. “You two make it look easy.”\n\n“It isn’t,” Bram said. “That’s why we do it.”\n\n“You mean that backwards,” Chloen said, grinning, and Bram ruffled her hair, which she suffered like a cat forced to enjoy affection.\n\nThey paced the catwalk. The Underface opened in a long ribbed hall, risers touching the spine at regular bars. The world above was work and shouting; down here was breath and song. A small beauty. Jhace gathered those where he could. The world had taken a lot and was greedy still; he let himself have a handful of small things when it wasn’t looking. The heat against his palm. Chalk dust on skin. The way Chloen’s questions came faster when she forgot to be ashamed of not knowing.\n\nBram fell into step beside him, boots hitting metal at the same cadence. “You working alone again tonight?”\n\n“We’ve got Junction seven to recheck,” Jhace said.\n\n“That’s not what I asked.”\n\nJhace gave him **~~silence~~** **silence;** it was easier than lying, and it made Bram do the talking he always wanted to do anyway.\n\n“You’re good with metal,” Bram said, gentling the words so they didn’t bite. “Less good with people.”\n\nJhace scrubbed chalk off his thumb. “Metal doesn’t leave.”\n\nThe quiet that followed had a weight to it, like a clamp you’d tightened too far. Bram fit words into it carefully. “Your parents didn’t leave you. They died.”\n\n“That’s a kind of leaving,” Jhace said, and the bitterness tasted like grit. He didn’t want Chloen hearing this, didn’t want his wound made into a training story. He pointed at the next riser to move them along. “Check the hanger bolts. Two spans apart, finger-true.”\n\nChloen went quick, grateful to be useful. Bram gave Jhace a look that said the talk wasn’t done, then let it be, because he was not a fool and the work took priority. Always the work.\n\nThey got three more risers in before the song went wrong enough that even Chloen heard it. The hum lifted half a pitch and held there like a breath someone forgot to release. Jhace’s arm hair rose. The copper taste sharpened until it felt like a coin under his tongue.\n\n“What—” Chloen began.\n\n“Stop,” Jhace said. Every word came out flat and square. He took the key back from her hand and set it down. “Don’t touch anything.”\n\nBram moved behind him and took a long slow breath—one, two, three, four, five—like he was counting how long it took panic to set. “Pressure’s climbing. Gauges say three heads and a half. Shouldn’t be.”\n\n“Where’s it feeding from?” Jhace asked.\n\n“Upstream,” Bram said. “Or skyward, if you like your nightmares literal.”\n\nThe light changed. Not brighter—thicker. As if someone had dipped the whole Underface in glass that hadn’t cooled yet. Shadows deepened by a hair, a strange quarter-turn twist to the air, an edge where a smooth should be. Jhace tasted the moment it tipped. He’d never had a word for it. He would later. For now it was that wrongness you feel when a beam you trusted shifts underfoot.\n\n“Back,” Jhace said. “Two strides. Hands on rail.”\n\nChloen obeyed without thought, bless her quick-heart. Bram didn’t move, because Bram was a stubborn old bastard and the grid was his and he’d never step back from it. Jhace grabbed his sleeve and pulled anyway, two spans of force, enough to make a point. Bram followed, but only because he chose to.\n\nThe hum became a tremor. Not in the risers—in the air. The Underface breathed in, walls a hand closer without moving, then held that breath too long. Jhace’s eyes watered. He blinked and the world wavered like heat above a quench pool.\n\n“What in the—” Bram began.\n\nThe tremor stacked, layer on layer, until the catwalk shivered under their boots. Chalk jittered in Jhace’s pocket. Far off, something groaned—steel talking in sentences no one wanted to translate. The pressure on the gauge crept past four heads, then the glass fogged with a white that wasn’t steam and the needle’s shadow doubled.\n\nJhace looked up.\n\nBetween the ribs of the Underface and the city skin above, the space had always been geometry and darkness, measured and known. Now it had a depth it wasn’t built to have. The shadow wasn’t a shadow. It was a weight. It gathered in a shape his mind refused and then sketched the refusal anyway.\n\nSomething massive was descending through the bones of the world.\n\nThe Underface smelled like limestone and old sweat. **<!--Line keeps motif cadence; end on sense detail-->**\n\n---\n\n### Chapter 2: Warped Horizon — (Tiffani POV)\n\nLight pooled in the glass like water that had forgotten how to fall. Tiffani set the sighting frame against the east pane and brought the horizon to a hair-true line across the crosshairs. The city below held steady—riser smoke, a few early carts, the Outer Crown throwing its ribs against the pale sky. The line did not hold. It bowed, then eased back, as if the world’s edge had surface tension and a thumb pressed it from the other side.\n\nShe blinked. The distortion stayed.\n\n“Frame two,” she said.\n\nMikel—junior, eager, quiet when reminded—passed the second frame without comment. He had learned not to ask what she saw until she told him how to see it. Tiffani braced the frame’s heel against the sill and adjusted the angle by half a finger, then another half, until the wire grid and the distant roofline came into clean coincidence. She held three breaths, counted a set, then checked the eastern rim again. Bow. Ease. A slow, uncommitted fracture of straightness.\n\nThe glass-house was all panes and ribs and clean walkway. No benches. **~~No glory-hole heat.~~** **No glory-hole heat**—just sightlines, measurement, records. Her kind of room. She noted the deviation in the ledger: “Watch 1, pre-Shaping. East horizon: concave deflection, one finger drop at two bars measured elevation. Repeatable.” She made a second mark: an underscore for uncertainty. Protocol.\n\n“Humidity,” Mikel offered, because he could not help himself.\n\n“Humidity makes the air a lens,” she said. “This is a hand too strong for humidity.”\n\nHe nodded like he understood and did not. That was fine. He would learn by standing beside, by arranging tools the way she asked, by watching her move and not moving wrong. Precision required a quiet field. People rippled the air.\n\n“Frame three,” she said.\n\nHe crossed the walkway quick as a trot and brought the angle-cage with the measuring cords wound tidy. She approved of that, silently. The cage was a spider of brass and string: eight spokes, each with a knot at a span, and a sliding bead for fine adjustment. She set it in the window’s corner where the mullions met at scribe-true ninety. The cage gave her four sightlines: cardinal compass, the city’s own plumb. She aligned the cords to the ribs of the Crown and to the far heat-rib that walked toward the edge of sight, then sighted through each open space in turn.\n\nFirst sight: clean. Second: clean. Third: a shimmer that could have been heat above a quench pool if the tide were hotter than breath-steam. Fourth: the same bowing line. The same reluctant return.\n\nShe wrote again. “Repeat deflection. Likely systematic.”\n\n“Systematic means we can solve it,” Mikel said, too hopeful.\n\n“Systematic means it is real,” she said. “Solving comes after knowing.”\n\nHe bit his lip, chastened. He was not wrong. He just liked the after better than the knowing. Most people did.\n\nTiffani switched windows. North had always been her favorite: clear line from the glass-house to the low slope and out across nearly two reaches of flat land before the ground rose again into the distant loom of other cities. Today, the north horizon bulged outward a finger and a half at the same elevation, as if the world’s pane had a bubble trapped inside it.\n\nShe swapped the cage for the plumb-sight—two bars of straight-edge with a drop-weight on a fine line, plumb-true when the weight stilled. She steadied her breathing. The weight steadied with her. The horizon did not. It lensed, bowed, settled, lensed again.\n\nA watch passed in work: four measurements in each direction, cords realigned between, panes wiped to avoid refraction from smudges, every number entered twice, once bold, once light. She stitched the data in her head as she wrote, **mapping** deflection like a crack propagating through a sheet: invisible at first, then everywhere once you taught your eye its shape. East and north bowed inward. South and west had an outward bulge that did not match wind or heat. The pattern was not pretty. \n\n“Do you want tea?” Mikel asked at last.\n\n“I want the world to stop bending,” she said, without heat. “Tea will not oblige it.”\n\nHe flushed and moved away, making himself useful with a rag on the panes. His reflection crossed the glass like a ghost. She did not look at him. She watched the distance.\n\n“This is not local,” she said after a long set, mostly to the ledger. “Not a furnace plume, not a vent. The curvature shifts with angle but remains centered on… not us.”\n\n“Could be a pressure change,” Mikel said, still hopeful, because pressure you could blame on something with a valve.\n\n“Then the birds would fly closer to ground,” she said, pointing with the pen where three specks cut their arcs clean. “They don’t.”\n\n“Oh.”\n\nHe was young, and not. People who came to the glass learned to become older than their bodies would claim. Tiffani had been twenty when Marisse took her hand and made her trace, with one finger, the exact line between almost and true. She could still feel the humiliation when she went a hair wide. She could still feel—**~~no~~** **no.** She folded the memory back and slid it into its proper slot. What the mind would widen to pain, the work pared away.\n\nMikel hovered. “Master Merrow?”\n\n“Do not call me that,” she said, easier than it felt. “Tiffani is sufficient.”\n\n“Yes. Tiffani.” He hesitated. “Why do you work alone up here?”\n\n“Fewer bodies to warm the air,” she said, eyes still on the horizon.\n\nHe waited.\n\nShe sighed. Precision was not always a numbers problem. Sometimes it was a tolerance you set around words. “Focus is a finite resource. Noise is a thief. People are—” She picked the line up by its clean edge, held it a breath, and set it down. “Imprecise.” **<!--Dynamics: Tiffani-Mikel distance probe per Bible; internal Marisse echo present-->**\n\nHe nodded like a student marking a note he would misread later. She wanted to tell him the truer thing—people leave, numbers don’t—but that belonged in the margin of a different ledger. He would not learn it from her saying so. He would measure it on his own.\n\nShe turned the east frame a half-hand to the left, then another half-hand, bringing a far tower into line with the frame’s upright. The tower wavered. Not shimmering, not the dance of heat. A slow, deliberate quiver as if someone had taken the whole bar of distance and twisted it a quarter-turn.\n\nTiffani’s neck prickled. The glass itself responded: a faint tick in the panes as the mullions sighed, a hairline song she felt through her fingertips on the sill. The sound did not carry; it pressed. The ledger’s ink line trembled as she wrote. The weight on the plumb-sight described a wider circle before it settled again.\n\nMikel went still in the way of animals.\n\n“What is it?” he whispered.\n\n“Something large,” she said. “Not here. Not far.”\n\nHe looked at her reflection. “Is that useful?”\n\n“It tells us whether to stand on the floor or under a brace,” she said. “Stand under a brace.”\n\nHe moved without argument to the rib where the glass-house’s bones crossed, and set his back against it. Tiffani kept her post at the window. The correct place to measure was not the safe place. Choosing otherwise gave you exquisite precision on the wrong thing.\n\nThe bowing line deepened. The deflection now matched a hand at two bars elevation—no, more, a hand and three fingers. The change outpaced the end of her last sentence. She started another and found there was no place to anchor it. The world’s skin went flexible and kept going.\n\n“Tell me where our closest proof-wall sits,” she said, mind already mapping stresses as if she could brace the horizon with a handful of glass struts.\n\n“Between the Outer Crown and the Foundry Run,” Mikel said, repeating lesson. “Two bars south of us. Why?”\n\n“If it fails, the lower districts will take the surge,” she said. “Look down. Look for pressure you can see.”\n\nHe edged to the window beside her. The city held, for now. Plumes rose. Cart wheels traced their lines. People moved at a walk; a few at a run. No one screamed. The world did not announce a catastrophe before it landed. That would be courteous. The world was not courteous.\n\nThe sound arrived as a pressure first. Not noise—a change in center. The panes took it, and the iron ribs passed it into the floor. Tiffani’s jaw ached. She swallowed and felt the swallow bounce.\n\nThen, past the north horizon, the air found its voice. A low roar, too large to fit in the ear, rolled toward them as if the world had begun to inhale and forgot where to stop. Birds broke their arcs midwing, recalculated, fled. The tower she had used for sighting now leaned, a finger only, then a hand, not by itself, but because the line between it and everything else had shortened.\n\nMikel cursed—short, honest. “What do we do?”\n\n“We measure,” she said, because answer and compulsion were the same. She set the frame for one more reading and kept her hand steady while the line bent. The deflection no longer crept; it surged. Her scribe-true hand could not track every rise. She marked center, edge, and returned. Three points define a circle. Triangulate the impossible and it becomes, at least, named.\n\nThe roar thickened until the glass-house sang at it, ribs humming in sympathy. Tiffani fixed the crosshairs on the bulge where the horizon had stopped pretending to be straight and said, to the ledger, to herself, to the future: “Final reading: horizon no longer a line.” **<!--Voice: geometrical cadence; balanced clauses-->**\n\nThe floor shivered. Somewhere below, a brace cried like a struck bell. Mikel grabbed the rib and held on.\n\nTiffani closed the ledger, set it under the sill, and rested both hands on the cool pane. Hairline songs ran under her palms, crossing, adding, canceling: interference made audible. She could map the fractures of sound even if she could not stop them. Precision in the face of force was not nothing. It was a promise to be ready when the world finished inhaling and whatever came next hit.\n\nThe roar kept coming. The glass pressed back. And the horizon—no longer a horizon—bulged outward as if the planet itself had been pierced from above and the pressure wave was the answer.\n\n**Heat made the measurement frame slick under her palms.** **<!--Motif: glass/heat; keep closing image-->**\n\n---\n\n### Chapter 3: Load Path — (Jhace POV)\n\n**~~Heat from the purge made the air shimmer like warped glass.~~** **Heat from the purge warped the air; glass-wrong.** **<!--Voice: punchier, Jhace metaphor family-->**\n\nThe Underface took the first breath wrong.\n\nGauges fogged white, then cleared to show needles not where needles belonged. The catwalk shuddered under Jhace’s boots, the vibration finding old bolts and making them remember how to squeal. The hum of the grid—his grid—shifted half a pitch and began to stack. Two songs on one wire. Nothing good ever came of harmony like that.\n\n“Hands on,” he said. “No hero work.”\n\nChloen already had a rail in her grip. Bram’s stance widened like a brace set in meat and stubborn. Far down the hall, a hanger bolt spat rust and the riser it held hopped, a finger only, and settled wrong. The air grew thick, light folding like glass at the edge of slump.\n\nThen the pressure arrived. Not steam—weight. It pressed the Underface in every direction at once, like a giant hand testing fit.\n\n“Junction 2-nine is going to complain,” Bram said, admirably calm for a man whose teeth were almost vibrating out of his skull. “Hear it?”\n\nJhace didn’t need to hear. He felt it. The saddle pins there had appetite; he’d fed them. They’d hold until they didn’t, and the failure would be loud.\n\n“Purge,” Jhace said. “We bleed four heads before the whole spine bows.”\n\nBram nodded. No argument. He grabbed the lanyard for the nearest purge bell and yanked. Far off, a bellman’s answer clanged once—ready. Chloen looked from man to man like waiting for permission to breathe.\n\n“**~~red hash means~~** **Red hash means** fix-order,” Jhace said, because she needed something to hold that wasn’t fear. “X-in-circle means done. Today we make new marks.”\n\n“What mark is this?”\n\n“Survive.”\n\n**Catwalk. Ladder.** He took the rungs at a clip, boots singing on steel, wrists already building a thrum that felt like someone knocking on his bones. The Underface groaned again, a long note, and the light thickened until the gauge glass showed two shadows where one needle should be.\n\nHe hit the purge gate for Segment East-Three, dropped the safety catch, and twisted the crown valve with his full weight. The wheel stuck a moment, then moved, then spun too fast. Heat breathed up at him, clean—not steam in the face, not yet. The riser tone dropped a hand. Good.\n\n“Next,” Bram barked, close behind.\n\nChloen came last, careful but not slow. The girl had good instincts; she kept her center low and her hands where they could live if the world decided to disagree.\n\n“E-Three is bleeding,” Jhace said. “We need West-One and South-Four to match or we’re pulling the spine out of square.”\n\n“You take South,” Bram said. “It’s your long legs that make the jump.”\n\nJhace didn’t argue. He went.\n\nSouth-Four meant crossing the sump run on a catwalk that had been a compromise when first laid and only gotten more honest about its personality since. Halfway across, something above them groaned and a fine drift of scale came down like gray snow. Chloen flinched. Jhace did not. He kept his eyes on the pins at the far end, because pins told you what mattered: appetite, grip, song.\n\nThe purge gate for South-Four had been painted in better tides. He kicked the latch loose and set the wheel to moving. Old paint cracked; new heat licked his knuckles. He didn’t pull away. He finished the spin, set the gate open, then backed his fingers out one by one, checking skin because he’d need it later. He would always need hands later.\n\nThe grid gave up a long sigh. Pressure dropped a half-head. The two songs slid closer to each other—still wrong, but less murderous.\n\n“Hold,” he said to the air like it could hear. The Underface shivered again, a ripple that ate distance and spat it back shorter. The catwalk underfoot went a hair out of level, rethought, and returned. Geometry should not think.\n\nBram’s voice rolled up the hall. “West-One’s sticky!”\n\nJhace ran to him. Sticky meant seized meant the wheel had married itself to its post and was now telling you about vows and permanence. Bram had a wrench on it and a look like he was willing to have a talk with the wheel’s mother.\n\n“Chloen,” Jhace said, pointing without taking his attention from metal. “Pry bar. The long one.”\n\nShe had it to his hand in three heartbeats. Jhace set the bar under the spoke and said to Bram, “On my pull.” Bram moved without comment, put his back to the wheel opposite Jhace’s bar, and braced his feet.\n\n“Three,” Jhace said. “Two. Pull.”\n\nThe wheel moved a stubborn finger. Jhace’s shoulder gritted. Something popped—bar or bone, he’d check later. They pulled again. The wheel turned a quarter. Heat breathed up through the purge stack and took the hair off Jhace’s forearm in one gust. This time he did pull back, not from pain, from calculation. Burn now meant clumsiness later. Clumsy killed.\n\n“Wrap,” Bram said, already throwing a rag around the wheel. It smoked. It was still better than bare skin.\n\n“Again,” Jhace said.\n\nThey got it open. West-One bled. The song flattened further, a relief that came with the small embarrassment of surviving this far. It was always embarrassing to have feared a thing and lived through it.\n\n“Look,” Chloen whispered.\n\nJhace looked where she pointed. Between the ribs, where the Underface left slivers of sight to the city’s skin, the world had done something he lacked words for. The space had deepened. The seams between plates looked longer. The light had been set to wrong. A shadow lay on the far wall where no object made it—just the idea of a weight given shade.\n\n“Down here isn’t supposed to have horizon,” Bram said.\n\n“It does today,” Jhace said. “Keep moving.”\n\nThey hit Junction 2-nine as it decided to make a speech. The saddle pin he’d fed earlier had developed a hunger you could see in the chalk mark—his mark, X inside a circle, clean and certain, now with a gap through the line that had not been there. Memory could lie. Chalk could not.\n\n“Back it a hair,” he said. “Then seat.” He put the key on the pin and tested. It was too free. Appetite again. He set his weight and pulled. The key slipped a tooth and barked his knuckles along the riser. He did not swear. He set again. Pulled again. The pin bit. The song in that corner of the grid went from tremor to hum.\n\n“Your marks are still your marks,” Bram said softly, as if he could measure doubt on a face the way gauges measured heads. “Don’t let the wrongness tell you stories.”\n\nJhace nodded like the motion could make it true, and chalked a new mark just above the first: X-in-circle plus a short bar across the top—double-checked under load. It would mean something to the next crew if there was a next crew.\n\nA bell clanged twice down the line. Not a purge bell. A proof call—there was a wall somewhere admitting it wasn’t sure it wanted to be a wall.\n\n“Outer Crown,” Bram said, listening with the old man’s ears that could read distance in metal.\n\n“Lower racks.”\n\n“Then they’ll be thinking about flow,” Jhace said, and the math ran in his head without numbers, all pressure and preference. “They’ll starve us a watch to save the Crown.”\n\n“Correct choice,” Bram said.\n\n“I wasn’t arguing.” Jhace wiped chalk on his trousers and tasted metal in the back of his mouth. Copper and something that wasn’t. “We give them what they need.”\n\nThey opened another bleed on North-Two just to take the edge off the shared spine. The grid groaned in gratitude. The wrongness in the air eased a fraction. Then the world decided easing was boring and pushed again.\n\nThe roar came like a beam sliding home into a notch cut the wrong size. Jhace felt it through his teeth, through the bones of his forearms against the riser, through the narrow old scars in his palms where work had made its maps. The catwalk went soft then stiff then soft again. The light stuttered. Far off, something failed with a clean bang and a spill of steam that ran under the hum like laughter at a funeral.\n\n“Brace,” Jhace said, though they were already braced. He put his back to a rib and put Chloen between him and metal. Bram took the outside because he would. The pressure tried to make three men into one thing. They refused.\n\nThe sound kept coming. The Underface answered. Ribs boomed in sympathy, a deep drum. The risers sang high over it, thin as wire.\n\nWhen the shove passed, the world did not return to the exact shape it had left. The grid’s song had a third note in it now, low and pulsed, something like a heart you didn’t consent to share a body with.\n\nChloen’s eyes were wide. “What is that?”\n\n“Not ours,” Bram said.\n\nJhace set his palm flat to the riser. The low pulse wasn’t pressure, not as he knew pressure. It was a beat under the beat. It found a cadence that made his own pulse trip for a breath, then picked a new one. He pulled his hand back like he’d touched a wire.\n\n“Topside,” he said. “I want eyes.”\n\nBram tipped his head. “You hate topside when it’s quiet.”\n\n“I hate guessing,” Jhace said. “We’re past what we can solve by listening at a pipe.”\n\n“You sure you want the girl—”\n\n“She comes,” Jhace said, sharper than he meant, then flattened his voice. “She needs to see how load moves when it stops being theory.” **<!--Dynamics: Jhace-Chloen nurture→inclusion seed; she’s brought into risk with purpose-->**\n\nChloen had the sense not to look pleased. She squared her shoulders and nodded.\n\nThey made fast marks as they went. Red hashes where the fix orders had multiplied, grind marks where a crew with more time would need to cut and reseat, X-in-circle where they’d brought appetite back to heel, double-bar over the worst of it to say this was checked under conditions no one had planned for.\n\nThey hit the ladder to the access hatch and climbed into a stairwell that had always smelled like cold brick and the soap someone’s aunt used to scrub it in tides when there were still assigned scrubs. Today it smelled like the inside of a bell.\n\nAt the hatch, Jhace put his ear to the plate. The pulse was there, too, the low third note behind the city’s noise. He unbarred the hatch and shoved the plate. It stuck a breath, then swung.\n\nLight hit them like a poured sheet. The sky was wrong in the way a straightedge is wrong when a child has chewed it. The Crown’s ribs leaned by a hand and then leaned back, not from wind, from idea. People stood in streets with their faces lifted and their mouths the shape of questions that would not matter.\n\nHalfway to the north horizon, the air bulged.\n\nJhace did not have Tiffani’s words for it. He had the ones he’d learned from risers and clamps. Load path. Failure mode. Yield. The bulge had chosen a path. It was moving down it.\n\n“Where’s our nearest brace?” he asked.\n\n“Two streets over,” Bram said. “Proof-wall gate.”\n\n“Then we help hold,” Jhace said. His skin prickled. The third note in the world’s song found his bones again and pressed. He pictured pins eating themselves and walls deciding to be honest. He pictured Chloen’s hands steady on a wheel because his were.\n\n“Move,” he said, and they moved into a city that had just learned how much it weighed.\n\n**<!--SLOT B INTRO: Quench Conflict Setup (delegation friction follows in Ch5 post-fix per Bible). Plant: Jhace insists on bringing Chloen; brief push from Bram shows future cost-conflict over who carries risk.       "children": [
        "b7c99319-884e-4047-89ce-86dfb834a093"
      ],
      "id": "e8f68356-0e71-4bc2-9150-5426aeac3ec9",
      "message": {
        "author": {
          "metadata": {},
          "name": null,
          "role": "assistant"
        },
        "channel": "final",
        "content": {
          "content_type": "text",
          "parts": [
            "Nice. These interludes absolutely slap thematically; they just needed a light buff, not a rebuild.\n\nHere they are with **light polish + tracked changes** (~~deletes~~, **bold adds**), no canon or structural changes:\n\n---\n\n## INTERLUDE I: Perfect and Pure\n\nCopper-gold warmth flooded his chest, then burned.  \n\n### PART A: CORLEXI—PERFECT LINES\n\nPrism was all angles pretending to be virtue. Corlexi loved it for that lie.\n\nHe stood on a ribbed gallery high in the half-built Spire of Measures, wind combed thin through the lattice, apprentices scattered below like commas in a sentence that would not end. Chalk lines webbed the floor. Plumb-threads hung perfectly still—not because the air was calm, but because he refused motion.\n\n“Again,” he said.\n\nThey lifted the truss and set it into the mouth of the waiting frame. Blue-silver threaded his fingers and stayed, a constant wire under the skin. He pressed it into the join—not hard, not soft, exactly—until the two faces of steel forgot their seam. The Spire’s hum shifted half a note closer to satisfied.\n\n“Master,” Adren ventured, careful. “We’ve been at it three watches.”\n\n“We are not tired,” Corlexi said, and made the statement feel true the way a line becomes true when enough force is applied. The boy straightened. Everyone did.\n\nA brace complained far below—soft, then quiet, chastened by a glance Corlexi did not bother to send. The city beyond lay sharpened by late glow, every roofline cutting the sky. He loved the certainty of edges. Loved the moment when a bracket’s petty war with gravity ended and the bracket conceded he had always been right. Disaster had a geometry; it could be designed against. If he perfected the forms, the world would remember how to hold itself.\n\nThey shifted the west run. Adren’s boot slipped on chalk and oil. He snatched at air, found nothing, tilted. Meve lunged, caught a sleeve, lost purchase. Corlexi moved a hand-width—no farther—caught Adren’s collar, kept the line of the truss with his other hand as if both tasks were one equation. Blue-silver whispered at his knuckles where they touched steel. The apprentice hung a breath over the drop, feet bicycling emptily.\n\n“Still,” Corlexi said, as if calming a beam. Adren froze. Corlexi set the boy upright without permitting the truss to skew a single finger off true. The metal sang compliance. He released the collar last.\n\n“Thank you, Master,” Adren said, scarce air and shame in it.\n\n“Do not thank me for maintaining form,” Corlexi said. “Thank the form for permitting you to remain inside it.”\n\nThey worked on. The glow that had begun as **an** event sat now like background, like breath. A pleasant pressure gathered in Corlexi’s knuckles; his fingers did not want to fold. He did not require them to. He shaped force through flat hands and let his wrists carry the line. Hands that lacked discipline shook; structures did not. He preferred structures.\n\nMeve squinted along the north run. “A hair over-stiff there. If wind takes a mood, we’ll get chatter.”\n\n“Remove the hair,” Corlexi said, “after we seat this.”\n\nBlue-silver settled into the truss like a clean number. The Spire answered with a long, low approval. Corlexi let himself breathe.\n\nHe imagined a city where every rib and brace and gutter held to this logic. Where bulges in the sky—whatever they were—could find no purchase because there were no irregularities to amplify. Where people lived inside certainty and forgot the taste of fear.\n\nHis thumb would not touch his forefinger. He watched the failure to meet with mild curiosity, as if it belonged to some other man left on his scaffold by mistake. He tried the other hand. Same result. Stiffness; bright, clean pain when he insisted. A ledger line, not a warning.\n\n“Water,” he said at last, magnanimous. “A swallow. Then the west truss.”\n\nRelief moved across the crew like wind over grass. He let it. They drank. They breathed. The blue-silver under his skin did not dim.\n\nPerfection **required** sacrifice. He had always been willing to pay.\n\n---\n\n### PART B: MOBEL—CLEANSING FIRE\n\nThe desert at Heattide stripped the polite off things. Mobel liked the honesty.\n\nHis tent was a shadow pinned to hard ground, canvas singing under a sun that refused to age. The woman on the pallet had a wound along the thigh that smelled like rot turned argumentative. Her husband knelt beside her with hope stretched thin between both hands, the way men hold rope when the beam is already failing.\n\nMobel washed his fingers in water he could have drunk and did not. He wiped them dry and worked with what he had: boiled cloth, clean band, a narrow iron warmed in coals until it went the color of admission. Heat has a voice; it speaks of endings. He let it speak through his hand.\n\n“Hold her,” he said.\n\nThe woman screamed once, clean and strong. Flesh bubbled, then flattened. The stink lifted and left something metallic and sharp behind. He walked the wound’s edge with care, burning away anything that argued with health. “Contamination requires fire,” Mobel said, as he said for all, a simple litany to steady hands. “Clean is the law.”\n\nOutside the tent, a knot of watchers held their breath in a single borrowed chest. They had followed him here—quiet women with hard faces; men with scars and eyes that had stopped searching for soft places; two youths too young to be certain and hungry for someone who seemed to be. They liked the clarity he offered. He liked that they liked it. It made the work simpler.\n\nHe bound the wound in boiled cloth. The woman sobbed into the cool and then, exhausted, slept. Her husband reached to kiss Mobel’s hands. Mobel lifted them out of reach. The skin had swollen from the iron—a pressure under the knuckles like water seeking a seam. He flexed and felt the complaint spread along the tendons like a fault line mapping itself. He filed it as inventory. Costs were comfort when counted correctly.\n\nA second couple pushed forward. The man’s forearm was banded with ink that crawled into an old cut. The skin around it went the wrong colors. “Please,” the woman said, throat hoarse. “We walked a reach.”\n\nMobel looked at the arm, then at the ink. “You kept filth in the skin,” he said, not loud. “You chose it.”\n\n“It’s my clan,” the man rasped. “Not filth.”\n\n“Then let your clan heal you,” Mobel said, and stepped aside. The watchers murmured—approval more than anger, relief at a rule applied. The woman begged again, soft, the way people do when their words have no leverage left.\n\nMobel did not compete with the desert. The desert corrected everything eventually.\n\nHe stepped into the sun because he liked the way heat made the ache in his hands feel less like pain and more like law. Wind picked up; the tent answered with a different song, one that had learned the low third note threading the world since the sky had bulged and passed. Mobel ignored it. His note was simpler: clean or not.\n\nHe looked east, where the horizon cut pure across sand, and thought of cities with their compromises. Thought of the way rot hid in corners and called itself neighborly. He rested swollen hands against the tent pole until the ache made a proper accounting.\n\n“Only the clean will stand,” he said. Words moved through the watchers like a heat-front, leaving belief behind in their wake.\n\nThen he went back in to boil more cloth, to pour more water he would not drink, to answer one more body with the fire it deserved.\n\n---\n\n## Interlude II: Rumor & Report\n\n### Salee—Everyone’s Pain\n\nCauldron-Hold called itself a clinic again because someone had wiped the ash off the sign. Salee didn’t argue with the name. Names kept people walking in. \n\nThey came in bent and left a little taller. That was the work. She took the worst of it. \n\nA line trailed out past the basins to the alley mouth—old burns that had learned to ache, fresh breaks that hadn’t learned manners, fevers that talked in riddles. She moved from pallet to pallet with a wet cloth and clean thread and the quiet voice that made people remember how to breathe. When her hand rested near a wound, the room tilted. Heat shifted. Pain leaned toward her like metal toward a magnet and then slid, obedient, under her skin. \n\nThe first man had a rib that wouldn’t let him breathe all the way. She set her palm near the bruise and listened. The tightness gathered like a fist and then unfurled itself into her. He exhaled to the bottom for the first time in a tide. “Bless you,” he said, astonished. She nodded and went to the next. \n\nBy watch’s end she had a tally of blessings, a basin of thread ends, and two forearms that had learned a new map: copper-brown lines writing themselves with neat malice, curving from palm-heels toward elbow’s bend. She washed them with the same cloth she used on other people and didn’t look long. Looking made the ache louder. \n\nA woman came in carrying a child who had not decided whether to stay. Salee took the weight, took the small hand, and let the room tilt again. The child’s eyes widened, then softened. The mother went to her knees and held Salee’s skirt like it was rope. \n\n“You’re taking it,” the mother whispered. \n\n“I’m borrowing it,” Salee said, which was the same lie she told herself. Pain didn’t return on its own. You had to hand it back, and she never remembered how. \n\nBetween patients, she checked the ledger. Not numbers; a list of names and short notes in a cramped hand: Marn—breath, Fex—hand, Old Juri—sleep. The list steadied her. It made the ache orderly. \n\nA boy with glass-shy in his palm flinched from the basin. “It will hurt,” he said, already ashamed. \n\n“It will,” she agreed. “Then it won’t.” She took his wrist, set her other hand near the glittering specks, and invited the sting to change direction. It came obediently, quick and bright. For a breath she couldn’t feel her own palm at all. When sensation returned, it was thinner, like light through milk. \n\n“Better?” she asked. \n\nHe nodded, surprised at his own courage, and left the basin grinning. She added **Ktt—shy** to the ledger. \n\nBy late watch, her hands trembled in a fine, irritating buzz. Someone offered her a cup. She smiled and forgot to drink it. People thanked her with bread, with buttons, with the way they stood up straighter. She stacked thanks on the shelf with the thread. The shelf sagged. \n\nAt the door, a man with a soldier’s old back asked, not unkindly, “What do you keep for yourself?” \n\nSalee looked at her palms—the lines a little darker now, climbing with diligent intent—and tried to recall the last time her body had complained in her own voice. The memory ducked out of reach like a shy animal. \n\n“I don’t remember what my pain feels like,” she said, and surprised herself with the truth of it. \n\nThe man bowed as if that were a sacred answer and left before she could take the weight of his gratitude. \n\nWhen the bell lied about rest, she turned the sign anyway. The alley grumbled; the city did not lack for injuries. A girl with fever stood already in the doorway, swaying. \n\nSalee raised her hands. The lines glowed dull and did not ask permission. \n\n“If I take all the pain,” she told the room, not looking to see who heard, “no one has to suffer.” No one argued. She went back to work. \n\n---\n\n### Eries—Endings Are Natural\n\nVenn Eries chose a quiet table beneath the temple’s unlit lamp and arranged his pages with care. Ink, straightedge, a neat stack of fools’ comfort. He called it a treatise to make the shaking in his fingers feel like industry. \n\nOn the Necessity of Endings. \n\nHe wrote the title again because the first time was untidy. Then a sentence that had been waiting behind his teeth since the Piercing: **Continuance without purpose is cruelty.** He paused, not for thought, but to measure the breath it took to commit the words to the page. Breath was a unit you could count; grief was not. \n\nNovices swept ash into orderly lines and avoided his table. The elders did not. They came in a cluster—polite, troubled, already decided. \n\n“Venn,” said the oldest, whose hands had buried more people than most storms. “You are scaring the mourners.” \n\n“I am consoling them,” Eries said, and put a period down with exact force. “I am teaching that endings are not failures. They are the mercy of design.” \n\n“A philosophy of surrender,” another said. \n\n“A philosophy of kindness,” Eries replied. He looked up and let them see how tired his eyes had grown in so few tides. “Release is a form of love. To force a grip past its honesty is violence.” They flinched at **“violence”**—a temple word, not to be used on priests. The oldest set a gentle palm on the page without smearing the ink. “You are dismissed from the hall,” he said, voice good at mercy. “Take your pages with you. Do not bring them back.” \n\nEjection did not feel like a fall. It felt like someone taking a weight off a beam already bowed. Eries gathered the treatise, left the lamp unlit, and walked into a street that had learned to speak in bells. \n\nBy the temple steps, three people waited as if they had known exactly when he would be thrown out. A woman with a healer’s hands and the tremor of someone who hadn’t slept. A man with a laborer’s shoulders wearing grief like a wet coat. A boy with ash on his eyelashes and no one’s hand to hold. \n\n“Are you Venn Eries?” the woman asked. The boy stared at the pages. \n\n“I am,” he said, surprised to hear his name still fit. \n\n“We heard you tell a woman to let go,” the man said, wary and hopeful in the same breath. “That she didn’t have to keep her father breathing through the night if there wasn’t any of him left to come back.” \n\n“She hit me,” Eries said, and found a smile that didn’t belong to cruelty. “Then she slept.” \n\nThe boy nodded like he understood something adults often hid from him. “Can you teach us,” he asked, “how to do… that? The letting?” \n\n“I can name it,” Eries said. “I can make it less frightening.” \n\nHe led them to a table that was not a temple’s. In the corner of a bread-shop that had decided to be a school for a watch, he opened the treatise and read the first paragraph aloud. People turned their heads; a few sat down without asking why. \n\nAs he spoke, his skin felt a size too large, like a garment you keep wearing for memory’s sake. The vitality leak was not dramatic—no gasp, no glamour—only a steady thinness, like pouring a jar in slow drops that never seemed to end. The woman’s eyes tracked it with the disgust of a practitioner who knew costs and hated to be grateful for them. \n\n“This will age you,” she said after the reading, blunt as duty. \n\n“It already has,” Eries said, and lifted his hand to show her the truth: veins a little darker, spots of chill under the skin where warmth didn’t bother staying. “Endings always do.” \n\nElders came to the shop door and hesitated—authority didn’t like rooms that named its limits. Passersby paused, listened, and found themselves sitting. A man who’d carried his mother’s body in his arms took the chair closest to the treatise and cried without apology. Eries handed him a cloth and kept reading. \n\n**Release is the kindest gift,** he wrote on a fresh page, to make sure the sentence would exist even if the bread-shop burned and the temple forgot him. \n\nSomeone asked from the back, scared and angry at once: “If everything ends, why build? Why love?” \n\n“Because endings make meaning,” Eries said, voice steady enough to carry. “Not less. More.” \n\nHe did not look back at the temple. He didn’t need its lamp. Outside, the city tested its braces and held, for now, at the edge of a proof it was not ready to see. Eries stacked his pages, felt the years add their quiet weight, and accepted the arithmetic. \n\n“All things end,” he told the room, and the room believed him because he looked the part. “Why fight it?” \n\n---\n\nIf you want, next step is slotting these cleanly:\n\n- **Interlude I** between **Ch5 and Ch6**  \n- **Interlude II** between **Ch10 and Ch11**\n\nI can also do a quick pass to mirror their titles/labels to your chapter headings and mini-audit formatting so the whole book feels like one seamless machine."
